PREFACE.

The present Edition of the Bh─Бmin─лvil─Бsa (I and IV)is meant primarily to meet the needs of studentsappearing for the F. Y. A. Examination in the year 1936.For the purposes of the text of this Edition, the NirnayaSagar Press Edition, the Calcutta Edition, and Mr. AyyerтАЩsEdition have been consulted. It is hoped that theIntroduction and the Notes will adequately meet therequirements of the F. Y. A. students, and lead them to anappreciation of the poetry of Jagannatha Pandit.

I have to thank my teacher and colleague, Prof.K. M. Shembavnekar, M. A., for the assistance whichhehas so readily given in the preparation of this Edition.Finally, thanks are due, and they are readily offered, tothe Management of the Bombay Vaibhava Press for thegeneral neatness of printing.

St. Xavier’s College,
Bombay.
1st. May, 1935

G. C. J.

INTRODUCTION

While paying a warm tribute, on one occasion, to thelate Lord Oxford (formerly Mr. Asquith) for his classicalknowledge and scholarship, Mr. Baldwin declared that hewas the last of the RomansтАФтАЬthe last of those whose debtwas to Athens and whose inspiration from Rome.тАЭItmay likewise be said of Pandit Jagann─Бtha that he wasthe last of the тАЬRomans,тАЭfor, the tradition of ClassicalSanskrit poetry which started practically with K─Бlid─Бsa,and which flourished through the vicissitudes ofseventeen centuries, found its last exponent of note inPandit Jagann─Бtha. This observation is not of courseintended to convey the impression that the tradition of Classical Sanskrit Poetry has ceased to exist altogether.Far from it. The fact is that while, on the one hand, itcannot be denied that eminent Pandits have proved themselves heirs to the tradition which Jagann─Бtha followedin his day, it has, on the other hand, to be admitted thatno successor of Jagann─Бtha has attained to a statureas great as his own; and, besides as time rolls on,thetradition is certain to be influenced and proportionatelymodified by foreign modes and currents of thought andexpression. Jagann─Бtha, therefore, may well be lookedupon as practically the last representative of the traditionof Classical Sanskrit Poetry.

HIS DATE.

The darkness hanging over Indian literary chronologyhas become proverbial. Though the assertion of Macdonellthat the Indian mind was lacking in the historical senseis to be treated as an exaggeration, yet it is not altogetherunfounded. About a great many of Sanskrit writers weknow next to nothing. In not a few cases, the dates can

approximately be proposed only in terms of centuries:and even so the dates proposed by different scholars differby centuries! K─Бlid─Бsa, according to some, might haveflourished in the First Century B. C., while, according toothers, he must have lived in the 4th Century A. D.Kauс╣нilya might be assigned either to the 4th century B. C.or the 3rd century A. D.; and so on. For such a sad stateof affairs, the writers themselves are indeed responsible.This aversion to autobiographical references on the part ofearlier writers like K─Бlid─Бsa seems to have been theresult of their natural orтАФwhat is more likelyтАФconventional reticence. The task of settling theirchronology therefore becomes proportionately difficult.

It seems however that the tradition in this respectunderwent a wholesome change, for the later Sanskritauthors do not show the aversion which their predecessorsdid. This modification has taken place early enough, forwe find B─Бс╣Зa making detailed personal references in hisрд╣рд░реНрд╖рдЪрд░рд┐рдд; and also, to a certain extent, in рдХрд╛рджрдореНрдмрд░реА; and B─Бс╣Зamight well have been, as Mr. Kane suggests, the first tobring about this wholesome change. Similarly, ┼Ъr─лHarsa, the author of the Naiс╣гadh─лya, who lived about theclose of the 12th century A. D., gives a tolerably goodaccount of himself and his works in the concluding stanzaof each Canto of his renowned K─Бvya. This conventionof making personal references attained greater and greatervogue, as can only be expected; yet, with all this changeit is not possible to arrive at precise dates about the birth,death etc. even in the case of later writers from whateverinformation they have left about themselves in theirworks.

Fortunately, Jagann─Бtha has been rather unstintingin making personal references. In a verse in the ┼Ъ─Бnta-

vil─Бsa. (45) he complacently says that during his youthhe enjoyed the splendid patronage of the Lord of Delhi:рджрд┐рд▓реНрд▓реАрд╡рд▓реНрд▓рднрдкрд╛рдгрд┐рдкрд▓реНрд▓рд╡рддрд▓реЗ рдиреАрддрдВ рдирд╡реАрдирдВ рд╡рдпрдГред Again, to the munificence of the Emperor he pays a warm tribute in thestanza :

рджрд┐рд▓реНрд▓реАрд╢реНрд╡рд░реЛ рд╡рд╛ рдЬрдЧрджреАрд╢реНрд╡рд░реЛ рд╡рд╛ рдордиреЛрд░рдерд╛рдиреН рдкреВрд░рдпрд┐рддреБрдВ рд╕рдорд░реНрдердГред
рдЕрдиреНрдпреИрд░реНрдиреГрдкрд╛рд▓реИрдГ рдкрд░рд┐рджреАрдпрдорд╛рдирдВ рд╢рд╛рдХрд╛рдп рд╡рд╛ рд╕реНрдпрд╛рд▓реНрд▓рд╡рдгрд╛рдп рд╡рд╛ рд╕реНрдпрд╛рддреНрее

Now, regarding the identity of this Lord of Delhi, there is a popular belief that it was Akbar who offered patronage tothe Pandit, and therefore Jagann─Бtha was a contemporaryof Akbar. On what evidence this popular view rests it isnot possible to ascertain. But, whatever that evidence, itis clear that it must be dismissed as unreliable becauseit would conflict with the internal evidence furnishedby Jagann─Бtha’s works, which unmistakably showsthat the Pandit flourished during the reigns ofthe Moghul Emperors Jehangir and Shah Jehan,particularly the latter. In the verse рд╢реНрдпрд╛рдордВ рдпрдЬреНрдЮреЛрдкрд╡реАрддрдВ1etc. Jagann─Бtha mentions рдиреБрд░реБрджреНрджреАрди which is areference to the Emperor Jehangir. And the highlylaudatory manner in which Jagann─Бtha refers toJehangir suggests that the Pandit must have enjoyedthe latterтАЩs patronage. The Emperor Shah Jehanis literally mentioned by Jagann─Бtha as his patron.Shah Jehan seems to have been so much pleased

HIS LIFE.

Jagann─Бtha was a Tailang Brahmin by caste, as isseen from the concluding verse of his рдкреНрд░рд╛рдгрд╛рднрд░рдгрдГ рддреИрд▓рдЩреНрдЧрд╛рдиреНрд╡рдпрдордЩреНрдЧрд▓рд╛рд▓рдпрдорд╣рд╛рд▓рдХреНрд╖реНрдореАрджрдпрд╛рд▓рд╛рд▓рд┐рддрдГ рд╢реНрд░реАрдорддреНрдкрд░рдорднрдЯреНрдЯрд╕реВрдиреБрд░рдирд┐рд╢рдВ рд╡рд┐рдЯреВрд▓реНрд▓рд▓рд╛рдЯрдВрддрдкрдГредрд╕рдВрддреБрд╖реНрдЯрдГ рдХрдорддрд╛рдзрд┐рдкрд╕реНрдп рдХрд╡рд┐рддрд╛рдорд╛рдХрд░реНрдгреНрдп рддрджреНрд╡рд░реНрдгрдирдВ рд╢реНрд░реАрдорддреНрдкрдгреНрдбрд┐рддрд░рд╛рдЬрдкрдгреНрдбрд┐рддрдЬрдЧрдиреНрдирд╛рдереЛ рд╡реНрдпрдзреНрдпрд╛рд╕реАрджрд┐рджрдореНрее In the opening sentences of рдЖрд╕рдлрд╡рд┐рд▓рд╛рд▓рд╕also which are quoted above, he refers to himself asрддреИрд▓рдЩреНрдЧрдХреБрд▓рд╛рд╡рддрдВрд╕реЗрди. Various episodes purporting to give interestingdetails in the life of Jagann─Бtha are current among thepeople; but none of them is corroborated by any unimpeachable external or internal evidence. One of suchepisodes persistently believed by the people would showthat while Jagann─Бtha was at the Court of the MoghulEmperor, he was smitten with love for a young Moslemdamsel whom, it is said, he ultimately married. Butthe tradition gains colour when we take into account theatmosphere at the Imperial Moghul Court. One point,however, is certain: The Pandit could not have marrieda yavana girl. This keeping of a yavani, too, must have madeJagann─Бtha a sinner and a derelict in the eyes of theBrahmins of Benares.

In later years of life, Jagann─Бtha seems to haveceased taking interest in worldly affairs, and bent onpassing the remainder of his days in piety and peace.From the 45th verse of the ┼Ъ─Бntavil─Бsaрд╕рдВрдкреНрд░рддреНрдпреБрдЮреНрдЭрд┐рддрд╡рд╛рдирдВрдордзреБрдкреБрд░реАрдордзреНрдпреЗ рд╣рд░рд┐рдГ рд╕реЗрд╡реНрдпрддреЗред it appears that he settled in рдордзреБрдкреБрд░реАi. e.рдордереБрд░рд╛, and passed his days in the devotion of Hari.This fact would cut right across the common belief thatthe Pandit passed his last days on the banks of theGangesтАФin K─Б┼Ыi. To this Jagann─Бtha gavethe brilliant retort that there was no danger to himbecause Mother Ganges was near by to take care of him.From this incident, it appears that Jagann─Бtha passed hislast days in K─Бsi. How then are these two mutuallydiscordant pieces of evidence to be explained? Itis open to us, in the last resort, to conclude, as it isalready suggested, that Jagann─Бtha may have lived forsome time both in K─Б┼Ы─л and in Mathur─Б in his last days.

JAGANN─АTHAAS A MAN.

Jagann─Бtha must have been a man of ambitions inlife, for, as we have already seen, after finishing hisstudies he left his native place and travelled abroad.His temperament seems to have been emotionallysensitive, as can be seen from his dealings with others.His attitude towards his parents is one not onlyof warm filial love but also of reverence. The parentswho had taken so much care to bring up their son wererewarded by the gratefulness and affection which thelatter cherished for them. To his patron, the Emperor,Jagann─Бtha bore high loyalty, which is shown by thewarmth of feeling with which the Emperor is referred toby him, whenever an occasion arises for him to do so.This emotional sensitivity bordered on impulsiveness.His emotional response to any stimulus was very great.And it is to this source indeed that are to be traced thewarm love and loyalty on the one hand, and the violentantagonism, on the other, both of which Jagann─Бthashowed. He was as loyal and friendly to his patron andothers as he was hostile to his foes. He undertook to writehis refutation of рдордиреЛрд░рдорд╛of Bhattoji Dixit mainly becauseBhattoji Dixit had turned тАЬfaithless to his GuruтАЭтАФanattitude which Jagann─Бtha could never understand, much

less connive at. With virtuous indignation and pride,he asserts: рдкрдгреНрдбрд┐рддреЗрдиреНрджреНрд░реЛ рдЬрдЧрдиреНрдирд╛рдердГ рд╕реНрдпрддрд┐ рдЧрд░реНрд╡рдВ рдЧреБрд░реБрджреНрд░реБрд╣рд╛рдореН. This sameemotional impulsiveness, again, explains the arrogant.looking personal references which Jagann─Бtha makes inhis writings. His scornful attitude towards other poets,his high estimation of his own abilities, the arrogantvein in his writingsтАФthese proceed from the impetuoustemperament of the emotional poet.

Yet, Jagann─Бtha was a scrupulous man. None but aconscientious heart could adopt such a touching attitudeas Jagann─Бtha did, when, laden with years and experienceof the world, he tried to look back upon the variedpageant of the past and take a review of how he hadlived all along: рд╢рд╛рд╕реНрддреНрд░рд╛рдгреНрдпрд╛рдХрд▓рд┐рддрд╛рдирд┐ рдирд┐рддреНрдпрд╡рд┐рдзрдпрдГ рд╕рд░реНрд╡реЗрд╜рдкрд┐ рд╕рдВрднрд╛рд╡рд┐рддрд╛рджрд┐рд▓реНрд▓реАрд╡рд▓реНрд▓рднрдк рдгрд┐рдкрд▓реНрд▓рд╡рддрд▓реЗ рдиреАрддрдВ рдирд╡реАрдирдВ рд╡рдпрдГред рд╕рдВрдкреНрд░рддреНрдпреБрдЬреНрдЭрд┐рддрд╡рд╛рд╕рдирдВ рдордзреБрдкреБрд░реАрдордзреНрдпреЗ рд╣рд░рд┐рдГрд╕реЗрд╡реНрдпрддреЗ рд╕рд░реНрд╡рдВ рдкрдгреНрдбрд┐рддрдЧрдЬрд░рд╛рдЬрд┐-рддрд┐рд▓рдХрдВрдирд╛рдХрд╛рд░рд┐ рд▓реЛрдХрд╛рдзрд┐рдХрдореНрее This verseshows how eager Jagann─Бtha was to shape hislife in accordance with the Vedic injunctions. It alsoshows the religious nature of Jagann─Бtha. As a matterof fact the devotional compositions like рдЧрдЩреНрдЧрд╛рд▓рд╣рд░реА, рдЕрдореГрддрд▓рд╣рд░реА,рдХрд░реБрдгрд╛рд▓рд╣рд░рд┐ all show the religious attitude of the poet. TheS─Бntavil─Бsa shows Jagann─Бtha as a fervent devotee ofViс╣г┼Жu, or ┼Ъr─лkс╣Ыsna. He was a Vaiс╣гс╣Зava of the Advaitaor ┼Ъ─Бnkara school.

JAGANN─АTHAтАЩS WORKS.

From among about a dozen compositions ofJagann─Бtha, there are four or five which are well-known,A reference is already made to the famous lyricalpoem рдЧрдЩреНрдЧрд╛рд▓рд╣рд░реАwhich is full of devotion and reverential emotion. The рдордиреЛрд░рдорд╛рдХреБрдЪрдорд░реНрджрд┐рдиреА was composed byJagann─Бtha with the set purpose, as the title itselfshows, of refuting the рдордиреЛрд░рдорд╛ in which Bhattoji

Dixit had propounded certain views contrary to thoseheld by ┼Ъeс╣гa┼Ыr─лkс╣Ыс╣гс╣Зa who was the Guru of Bhattoji Dixit.The рдЪрд┐рддреНрд░рдореАрдорд╛рдВрд╕рд╛рдЦрдгреНрдбрдирдореН is a work on Rhetoric and iscomposed in order to point out the faults in рдЪрд┐рддреНрд░рдореАрдорд╛рдВрд╕рд╛, acomposition by Appayya Dixit. But by far better knownand looked upon as a standard work on Rhetoric is theрд░рд╕рдЧрдЩреНрдЧрд╛рдзрд░. In this work Jagann─Бtha examines and refutesthe views of his illustrious predecessors and expounds hisown. While this work shows the masterly knowledge ofJagann─Бtha of the science of Poetics, it shows him at thesame time as an able writer both in prose and verse.His prose is characterized, as Prof. Kane has well observed,by a lucid and vigorous style and displays great criticalacumen. In his exposition of a topic, Jagann─Бtha hadto cite examples. But, conscious as he was of his ownpoetic powers, he disdained to draw upon others andcomposed his own verses suitable to the topic undertreatment. With great elation he says in the beginningof the рд░рд╕рдЧрдЩреНрдЧрд╛рдзрд░ :тАУрдирд┐рд░реНрдорд╛рдп рдиреВрддрдирдореБрджрд╛рд╣рд░рдгрд╛рдиреБрд░реВрдкрдВ рдХрд╛рд╡реНрдпрдВ рдордпрд╛рддреНрд░ рдирд┐рд╣рд┐рддрдВ рдирдкрд░рд╕реНрдп рдХрд┐рдЮреНрдЪрд┐рддреНред рдХрд┐рдВ рд╕реЗрд╡реНрдпрддреЗ рд╕реБрдордирд╕рд╛рдВ рдордирд╕рд╛рдкрд┐ рдЧрдиреНрдзрдГ рдХрд╕реНрддреВрд░рд┐рдХрд╛рдЬрдирдирд╢рдХреНрддрд┐рднреГрддрд╛.рдореГрдЧреЗрдгрее His stanzas bear testimony to the poetical abilityof the Pandit. тАЬThey are composed in an easy, flowingand graceful style and exhibit great poetic talentтАЭ. Theрднрд╛рдорд┐рдиреАрд╡рд┐рд▓рд╛рд╕ is a poetical composition which is alsowell-known. There is aspecial quality of his poems which Jagann─Бtha refers tonow and then in the concluding stanzas of the ┼Ъ─Бntavilasa,and on which he prides himself. It is рдорд╛рд╛рдзреБрд░реНрдп. In hisopinion (cf. ┼Ъ─Бntavil─Бsa, 42), a poetical composition is

worth its name only if this quality, рдорд╛рдзреБрд░реНрдп, is present in itрдорд╛рдзреБрд░реНрдпis one of the ten qualities of рдХрд╛рд╡реНрдп and partakes of atwofold natureтАФрд╢рд╛рдмреНрджand рдЖрд░реНрде. The тАШVerbal SweetnessтАЩ(рд╢рд╛рдмреНрджрдорд╛рдзреБрд░реНрдп) is defined by Jagann─Бtha as: рд╕рдВрдпреЛрдЧрдкрд░рд╣реНрд╕реНрд╡рд╛рддрд┐рд░рд┐рдХреНрддрд╡рд░реНрдгрдШрдЯрд┐рддрддреНрд╡реЗ рд╕рддрд┐ рдкреГрдердХреНрдкрджрддреНрд╡рдВ рдорд╛рдзреБрд░реНрдпрдоредтАШVerbal SweetnessтАЩisgained by the use of separate words while at the sametime avoiding short syllables followed by conjuncts.And the тАШSweetness of senseтАЩ (рдЖрд░реНрдердорд╛рдзреБрд░реНрдп) is defined as:**рдПрдХрд╕реНрдпрд╛ рдПрд╡реЛрдХреНрддреЗрд░реНрднрдЩреНрдЧрдпрдиреНрддрд░реЗрдг рдкреБрдирдГ рдХрдердирд╛рддреНрдордХрдореБрдХреНрддрд┐рд╡реИрдЪрд┐рддреНрд░реНрдпрдВ рдорд╛рдзреБрд░реНрдпрдореНред**TheтАШSweetness of senseтАЩis the variety of expressing one andthe same idea in different charming ways. The рднрд╛рдорд┐рдиреАрд╡рд┐рд▓рд╛рд╕, of course, is characterized by this two-fold **рдорд╛рдзреБрд░реНрдп.**and Jagann─БthaтАЩs claim has to be conceded.

However, attention must be drawn to certain drawbacks in Jagann─Бtha’s poetry from our own point of view.One such is his mannerisms. Sometimes he prefers anawkward way of expressing an idea to a direct andsimple expression of the same. An illustration in pointis the use of the expression рдкреБрд╖реНрдЯрд┐рдВ рддрдиреН in the line **рдпрддреНрдкрд┐рд╖рддрд╛рдордкрд┐рдиреГреГрдгрд╛рдВ рдкрд┐рд╖реНрдЯреЛрд╜рдкрд┐ рддрдиреЛрд╖рд┐ рдкрд░рд┐рдорд▓реИрдГ рдкреБрд╖реНрдЯрд┐рдореНред**He means to say, тАШThoudelightest with thy fragrance etc.тАЩ But instead of directlysaying so, he uses the rather awkward phrase рдкреБрд╖реНрдЯрд┐рдВ рддрдиреЛрд╖рд┐,тАШThou dost bring about the nourishment or satisfactionresulting therefromтАЩ. рд╕рдВрддрд╛рдкрдорд╛рд▓рд╛in vs. 15 in the Pr┼лst─Бvikavil─Бsa is another phrase showing the same drawback.It is not difficult to perceive why Jagann─Бtha used thephrase рдкреБрд╖реНрдЯрд┐рддрдиреНin the context. The reason is, and thatshows another drawback, Jagann─Бtha’s great love foralliteration. This passionate love for an alliterativeeffect is responsible for the occasional use of roundabout expressions. In fairness to the Pandit, however, itmust be noted that much of whatever artificiality is seen

in his poetry is to be attributed to the literary conventionscurrent in his time.

BH─АMIN─кVIL─АSA.

It is not possible to say exactly what the title wasmeant by the Pandit to convey. It is surmised that theBh─Бmin─лvil─Бsa was composed by Jagann─Бtha for thedelight and edification of his wife whose name, it is tobe supposed, was рднрд╛рдорд┐рдиреА. The word, **рднрд╛рдорд┐рдиреА,occurs severaltimes in the рднрд╛. рд╡рд╛. e.g., in the opening verse of theрд╢реГрдЩреНрдЧрд╛рд░рд╡рд┐рд▓реАрд╕;also in the 3rd and 17th stanzas of theрдХрд░реБрдгрд╡рд┐рд▓рд╛рд╕.**On the contrary, it may be supposed that thework is so called because Jagann─Бtha has therein,especially in the рд╢реГрдЩреНрдЧрд╛рд░рд╡рд┐рд▓рд╛рд╕, described the graces of abeautiful woman. The purpose for which the Bh─Бmin─лvil├дsa was тАШcomposedтАЩis altogether different, as thepoet says in the last verse of the ┼Ъ─Бntavil─Бsa. Hemakes no mention of рднрдорд┐рдиреА in this connection, butsimply says that he wanted thereby to preserve hispoetical gems from plagiarists.

The Bh─Бmin─лvilasa consists of four vil─Бsas, out ofwhich the first and the last are edited here. Thereseems to be no vital connection between the variouscilasas except the fact that the second and the thirdcilasas describe a beautiful woman from different pointsof view. There is a certain unity of nature in thecontents of the three vil─Бsas except the first which, likethe whole work, is an anthology. In the Prastavikavilasathe poet praises good men, censures the wicked, eulogizesvirtues like genosity, helpfulness, etc., draws attention tothe changing wheel of Fortune-indeed, he looks at Lifeas it is, and bodies forth his impressions in the form ofverses. In the other vilasas, on the other hand, there isa respective unity of theme which is treated in an ample,varied way.

15 тАЬWhen I am dried up by hundreds of theflaming rays of the summer-sun, whom will the streamof travellers, harrassed by the great scorching heat,approach?тАЭтАФBlessed is the life (or water) of the pondby the way-side, whose corpus steadily (lit. always)wastes away under the unceasing stress of such thoughts.Fie on the birth of the oceans!

16 While thou art becoming contracted (i. e.while thy water is drying up),O lake, the birds on allsides have flown up into the sky, and the bees are(now) betaking themselves to the mango-sprouts. Butto what alternative, alas, can the poor pitiable fishhave recourse?

17 In this wind as in the bee, do not presume thegreed for fragrance, O lotus-plant. Only for the sakeof the joy of the people has he allowed his respected selfto be a supplicant.

18 Do not, O M─Бlati Creeper, treat the sweetlyhumming bee coldly; the highly generous celestial treesbear him respectfully on their Leads.

19 O Sandal tree, thon Dearest even those serpentsby whom thou, endowed as thou art with a number ogood qualities, art rendered unenjoyable for good men!How shall we (adequately) eulogize (this) thymagnanimity?

20 The whole of the forest was ransacked, and allthe trees around were examined. But, O mango-tree,thy equal was not found by the bee.

21 If, having placed thy foot on (i. e. havingenjoyed) the flower of the celestial tree, which farexcels (lit. which has brushed aside all talk of) otherfragrance, thou dost desire, o bee, to move to any otherflower-well, blessed art thou, then!

22 Ponder well (lit. for long). O River, whetherit is meet for thee, rising from the Vindhya mountainand (therefore) pure, to receive the (filthy) streetwater even while thou art drying up?

23 OBabbul tree, tell us for what purpose wemay approach youтАУyou who are never seen adornedwith leaves, fruits or flowers, but (on the contrary)are always aтАУbristle with thorns?

24 Being alone (of the species) in this forest, OCuckoo, do not give out thy coos. (For) These ruthless crows do not hunt thee down through their mistaken belief in the commonness of species (between theeand them).

25 What quality has driven you, OFather ofBhav├бn─л(i. e. the Himalaya mountain), to bear thismass of snow which robs all trees of their charmingbeauty, and which brings distress to the beings in theworld?

26 O young elephant, never should you scorn thisbee which has approached you. For, he is worthy tobe carried on the head even by the best elephantscharming on account of the flow of rut.

36 On hearing his terrible rumbles in front of you,do not at all be disturbed in mind, O traveller. Haveyou not heard, O friend, about this water-bearer (cloud)that has dedicated his life (water) to the relief of thedistress of the world?

52 Oprince among elephants, don’t you moveabout the mountain caves, being elated with excessivepride. For, if the sucking cub of the lion awakes, theearth will have only female-elephants left on it.

53 The expert gardener. accomplished in (the artof) well-planting the various trees, instinctively (i. e.without caring to think) planted the Bakula, too, inan odd corner. But who knows that the Bakula, thusstowed away into a corner, will fill the whole worldwith the fragrance of the bunches of its flowers?

54 While the great sea-monster (Raghava) playedabout, the quarter-elephants were led, by the greatnoise of the waves dashing all round, in their hearts tobelieve, through mistake, that it was the rotation ofthe churning mountainтАФon whose breast should thissea-monster, who is well-versed in the art of swallowing the bodies of large fish, disport himself now thathe has left the ocean on account of a love-quarrel?

61 What valour should the lion, who claims thecleverness in scratching the itch on the temples ofelephants. proclaim before herds of deer?

62 Thy birth (is) from clear water; thy beautyrivals that of the face of a beautiful woman; thyresidence, too, (is) in the hand of Hari ; and thy perfumecaptivates the hearts of the gods. Therefore thou artthe precis of the words of great poets and the all-in-allof Cupid, o lotus. If thou shouldst conceive attachment to the honey-sucker, what shall we say to thee?

63 O best among elephants, why dost thou liedown comfortably, with eyes playfully closed? (Thoushouldst know that) enmity with the lion who is in theend dreadful (only) grows.

64 Never do words rashly escape the lips of thewise. And once escaped, they never go back like thetusks of elephants.

105 On hearing the low thunder (of the cloud), the cub of a lion, born just half a night before, contracts his limbs in order to bound forth, and (in so doing) conceals himself in his mother’s lap.

26 Even though it perceives many men entering the mouth of death every moment, yet why. alas, does the mind not turn away from objects of pleasure even now?

28 The enemy may steadily fan a great mass of fire over my head: he may even heave a swordтАУedge at me. But I shall never indulge even in a grain of censure (or falsehood).

30 May I never come even for a moment topossess wealth which looks charming on account of thesweet buzz of swarms of bees hovering about the ichorof the flocks of rutting elephants. This Vil─Бsa is calledIntroductory, presumably, because the Bh─Бmin─лvil─Бsa openswith it. At the same time it is to be noted that there is nointegral connection between this Vil─Бsa and the others thatfollow it. Indeed, the commentator does not call itрдкреНрд░рд╛рд╕реНрддрд╛рд╡рд┐рдХрд╡рд┐рд▓рд╛рд╕, but the **рдЕрдиреНрдпреЛрдХреНрддреНрдпреБрд▓реНрд▓рд╛рд╕тАФ**a title certainly more to thepoint, inasmuch as it carries with it a general hint about thenature of its contents. Another point to be noted in thisconnection is that the title, whichever of the two is accepted, ofthis vil─Бsa, does not conform to the uniformity which is evidentin the titles of other vil─Бsas. While the subsquent vil─Бsasderive their titles from the sentiment prevailing throughout theverses contained in them, this vil─Бsa, in the first place, has nosuch individual sentiment predominant in it; and, secondly, thetitle is not therefore connected with any sentiment whatever.рд╡рд┐рд▓рд╛рд╕тАУfrom the rt. рд▓рд╕реН with рд╡рд┐, to shine, or to play.

Translation.тАФPoetry is nectar, indeed, on earth for it isthe highest water-mark of sweetness, it is produced (likenectar which arose from the milky ocean when it was churned)by the churning of the ocean of Learning, and because it givesmuch happiness to the тАШdrinkersтАЩ.

  1. The substitution of рдордоin place of рдирдиреБcan hardly becountenanced in view of the fact that **рдирд╛рдЧреЗрд╢рднрдЯреНрдЯ,**who was removed from Jagann─Бtha by less than two generations, reads рдирдиреБinhis commentary on this verse in the R. G.

from the concluding ones of Bh─Бmin─лvil─Бsa as well. The poetwho is careful enough to add more than half a dozen stanzasof personal eulogistic references at the end of the ┼Ъ─Бntavil─Бsa,and who avers that he has culled these verses of his togetherto keep them safe from literary pirates may certainly be expected not to open рдЧреНрд░рдиреНрдеhis merely with a verse of such dubiousrelevance. The characteristic opening of Jagann─Бtha is vigorous and rather ampleтАФboth of which qualities are certainlyabsent in the present case.

It seems, therefore, that Jagann─Бtha has here infringed theestablished tradition which otherwise he follows. Probably,this breach of tradition is to be attributed to the fact that theBh─Бmin─лvil─Бsa is not an integral composition. As Jagann─Бthaadmits in the last stanza of the ┼Ъ─Бntavil─Бsa, the Bh─Бmin─лvil─Бsa is an anthology of the verses which the poet had composed and which he wanted to preserve from plagiarists. Onaccount of this promiscuous nature of its contents, Jagann─Бtha does not seem to have thought it his worth while toendow the Bh─Бmin─лvil─Бsa with his characteristic openingVerses.

Commentatorial ingenuity, however, has been too wideawake to allow the charge of a breach of tradition to rest onJagann─БthaтАЩs head. Consequently, we are told by thecommentator рдЕрдЪреНрдпреБрддрд░рд╛рдп that the verse рджрд┐рдЧрдиреНрддреЗ рд╢реНрд░реВрдпрдиреНрддреЗ etc. is itselfaрдордЩреНрдЧрд▓ of the рд╡рд╕реНрддреБрдирд┐рд░реНрджреЗрд╢рд╛рддреНрдордХ type, and, as such, it yields no lessthan three different interpretations! According to himрдореГрдЧрдкрддрд┐ stands for (i) the рдиреГрд╕рд┐рдВрд╣ incarnation of рд╡рд┐рд╖реНрдгреБ; or (ii)рдкрд░рд╢реБрд░рд╛рдо who protected the Brahmins seeking (рдореГрдЧ) truth; or(iii)рдХреГрд╖реНрдг who is the husband (рдкрддрд┐) of Gopis and acts likea deer (рдореГрдЧрд╡рддреНрдкрддрд┐рдГ) for he roams about in the forest!9 All these,

cause is understood from an effect, e. g. in 73, 52 below (рдЧрд┐рд░рд┐рдЧрд╣реНрд╡рд░реЗрд╖реБ etc.) in the last p─Бda it is said тАШthe earth will have onlythe female-elephants left behindтАЩ, though what is really intended is тАШthe cub of the lion will devour youтАЩ. Here the causeis conveyed through the mention of the effect. iv. When aneffect is conveyed through the mention of a cause, e. g., in vs.51 below (рд╕реНрдерд┐рддрд┐рдВ рдиреЛ рд░реЗ рджрдзреНрдпрд╛etc.), the elephant is asked not to stayin that forest region, for the lion, the enemy of elephants, issleeping nearby. From this it is meant that if the lion, beingawake, finds the elephant there, he will surely kill him. Here,therefore, an effect is understood from the mention of a cause;and lastly, v., when a thing is conveyed through the description of another similar thing, for instance, in the presentverse (рджрд┐рдЧрдиреНрддреЗ etc.).

In the present verse the poet’s intention is not indeedto convey the merely literal meaning. Obviously, he wishesto speak of a very powerful individualтАФor, probably about hisown self as an unrivalled poetтАФwho finds no worthy opponent,and disdains to massacre the innocent or the weak. Therefore,he stands superior to all, though yearning for a worthy fighter.Instead of saying all this in so many words, the author drawsupon the analogy of a lionтАФand thus succeeds in driving hispoint home in an effective, though indirect, way.

As a matter of fact, almost all the verses in this vil─Бsacontain the figure рдЕрдкреНрд░рд╕реНрддреБрддрдкреНрд░рд╢рдВрд╕рд╛and therefore mean more thanwhat meets the eye.

Vs. 16тАФрдкрддрдЩреНрдЧ A bird. **рд░рдорд╛рд▓рдореБрдХреБрд▓рд╛рдирд┐тАУ**The tender sprouts onthe mangoтАУtree. рддреНрд╡рдпрд┐ рд╕рдВрдХреЗрдЪрдордЮреНрдЪрддрд┐-When thou art thinned i. e.when thou art dried up. When, however, the lake driesup, and the lotuses fade, they unscrupulously migrate toanother more congenial resort-the mango-tree, as the poet sayshere. The bees and the birds are only casual visitors of the lake:they stay as long as it suits them. The fish, on the otherhand, is a habitual dweller in the lake. Its plight certainlybecomes tragic when the lake dries, for it cannot shift to anyother place.

The poet expresses the dilemma: whether to praise a greatman for his good qualities, or to despise him for his bad traits,both of which e, like all others, possesses. Preference toeither would betray partiality or prejudice; overlooking oraccepting both only shelves, not solves, the question. A knottyproblem indeed! (though, it must be admitted, very few wilfail to make up their minds in practice).

Vs. 85.**тАФрдЕрдЪреНрдЫрд╛тАУClear. A clear head is well called by thepoet & better possession than ordinary jewels. рджреНрд╡реИрддрд╛рдиреНрдзрдХрд╛рд░реЛрджрдпрдГтАФThe rising darkness of Duality. According to Advaita Philosophy, the only thing that really exists in рд╡реНрд░рд╣реНрдордиреН, everythingelse being non-existent, only an illusion brought about myprimary Nescience. The differences & distinctions of phenomenal existence are therefore unreal, and misleading. HenceрджреНрд╡реИрдд(Duality) is called тАШdarknessтАЩbecause it does not reallyexist. рдХрд┐рдВ рд╢рд╛рд╕реНрддреНрд░рдореН etc.тАФThe real ┼Ъ─Бstra is that which dispels thedarkness of тАШDualityтАЩand enables one to realize theрд╕рд░реНрд╡рд╛рддреНрдорднрд╛рд╡.рддрддреНрддреНрд╡рд╛рд╡рд╡реЛрдзтАФ**Knowledge of facts. Vs. 128.тАФрдЦрдгреНрдбрд┐рддрд╛рдиреЗрддреНрд░ etc.тАУрдЦрдгреНрдбрд┐рддрд╛рдирд╛рдВ рдиреЗрддреНрд░рд╛рдгреНрдпреЗрд╡ рдХрдЮреНрдЬрд╛рдирд┐ (рдкрджреНрдорд╛рдирд┐), рддреЗрд╖рд╛рдВ рдЖрд▓реАрдирд╛рдВ рдордЮреНрдЬреБрд░рдЮреНрдЬрдиреЗ (рдордзреБрд░рдкреНрд░рд╕рд╛рджрдирдХрд░реНрдордгрд┐) рдкрдгреНрдбрд┐рддрд╛рдГред Expert in sweetly pleasing (or opening) the clusters of lotuses in the form of the eyes of women supplanted in love. рдкрджреНрдорд╜ open out when the sun rises, cf. **рдкрджреНрдорд╛рдХрд░рдВ рджрд┐рдирдХрд░реЛ рд╡рд┐рдХрдЪреАрдХрд░реЛрддрд┐ред**Bhar. 1-74. S─АNTAVILASA.

** рд╢рд╛рдиреНрддрд╡рд┐рд▓рд╛рд╕тАФLike the two Vil─Бsas which immediately precede itтАФрд╢реНрд░реГрдЩреНрдЧрд╛рд░рд╡рд┐рд▓рд╛рд╕** and рдХрд░реБрдгрд╡рд┐рд▓рд╛рд╕, this Vil─Бsa too derives its name after one of the Rasas, though it is to be noted that рд╢рд╛рдиреНрддis not admitted as an independent Rasa by all schools of Sanskrit rhetoricians. Like the other two, again, the title of this vil─Бsa serves as an index of the sentiment prevailing throughout most of the verses comprising it.

** рд╢рд╛рдиреНрддрд░рд╕** is the sentiment of Quietism. When the battles of life have been waged and their stark horrible nakedness is perceived, and when, consequently, the dust and the rear of the Battle. so inviting and irresistible at other times, lose their appeal, the mind, becoming tired of worldly pursuits and the arge of senses, becomes chastened; a tranquil mood supervenes; and one yearns to rise above the Babel and escape into that Full Life which is all tranquillity. Thus the sentiment of QuietismтАФof the peace of mindтАФinduces in the end an atmosphere as placid and untroubled as some forest retreat, untrod by man, where the morning sun shines over the softly bubbling water of the stream. In this Vilasa, the poet depicts a frame of mind to which worldly pleasures appear to be so many fetters, senseтАФorgans so many hoops, life beset with its untold worries an oppressionтАФthe whole world a horrible nightmare. To such an harassed soul the poet points out the way of escape from misery, Viz., the worship of ┼Ъr─л Kс╣Ыс╣гс╣Зa who being identical with the highest Divinity is certain to rescue the miserable and worried refugee from his difficulties. In most of the stanzas of this vil─Бsa, therefore, in spite of the varied approaches to the theme, there is but one refrain, and that is the advice to all to dedicate their lives at the feet of ┼Ъr─л Kс╣Ыс╣гс╣Зa. And Jagann─Бtha, like a devout Vaiс╣гс╣Зava, waxes passionately devotional in his reverential references to, or descriptions of, that fascinating divinity-┼Ъr─лKс╣Ыс╣гс╣Зa.

Vs. 1**.тАФрд╡рд┐рд╢рд╛рд▓тАжрд╡рд┐рдХрд▓рд┐рддрдореНтАУрд╡рд┐рд╢рд╛рд▓рдВ рдЪ рддрджреН рд╡рд┐рд╖рдпрд╛рдЯрд╡реАрд╡рд▓рдпрдореН, рддрд╕реНрдорд┐рдиреН рд▓рдЧреНрдиреЛ рдпреЛ рджрд╛рд╡рд╛рдирд▓рд╕реНрддрд╕реНрдп рдкреНрд░рд╕реГрддреНрд╡рд░рд╛рдГ(рдкреНрд░рд╕рд░рдгрд╢реАрд▓рд╛рдГ) рд╢рд┐рдЦрд╛рдГ, рддрд╛рд╕рд╛рдВ рдЖрд╡рд▓реАрднрд┐рдГ рд╡рд┐рдХрд▓рд┐рдирдореНред рд╡рд┐рд╖рдпрд╛рдГтАФрд╡рд┐рд╢реЗрд╖реЗрдг рд╕рд┐рдиреНрд╡рдиреНрддреАрддрд┐ рд╡рд┐рд╖рдпрд╛рдГ**Those that fetter the mind and the soul more and more i. e. worldly pleasures or objects.**рдЕрдЯрд╡рд┐рд╡рд▓рдореНтАУ**forest region. Worldly pleasures are fraught with as many dangers as a forest region is. For, from the philosophical point of view, they try to prevent the soul from realizing its own true nature; and until the soul realizes the truth it will have perforce to journey on in the Sans─Бra. The story is told in order to glorify the power of the name ofViс╣гс╣Зu or N─Бr─Бyaс╣Зa. The story may be summarised as follows:Aj─Бmila was a Brahmin living in K─Бnyakubja. He had become depraved by associating with a slave-girl. He had tensons, the youngest of whom, N─Бr─Бyaс╣Зa by name, was verydear to the parents. Vs. 15тАФThe poet now shows the greatness and the powerof the name of Kс╣Ыс╣гс╣Зa, **рд╡рдЬреНрд░рдВрдВ рдкрд╛рдкрдорд╣реАрднреГрддрд╛рдореНтАФ**Just as the adamant ofIndra is the deadly enemy of mountains (See notes on Pr─Бst─Бvi k.Vs. 86), so is the name of Kс╣Ыс╣гс╣Зa to mountains of sins. рднрд╡рдЧрджреЛрджреНрд░реЗрдХтАФрднрд╡рдПрд╡ рдЧрджрдГ(рд░реЛрдЧрдГ) рддрд╕реНрдп рдЙрджреНрд░реЗрдХрдГ (рдЖрдзрд┐рдХреНрдпрдореН)редAcute disease inthe form of worldly existence. рдорд┐рдереНрдпрд╛рдЬреНрдЮрд╛рди etc.тАФрдорд┐рдереНрдпрд╛рдЬреНрдЮрд╛рдирдореЗрд╖ рдирд┐рд╢рд╛рддрд╕реНрдпрд╛ рд╡рд┐рд╢рд╛рд▓рдВ рдЪ рддрддреНрддрдордГред The pitchy darkness of the night of IgnoThe position is this: Brahman alone is real; the worldIs illusory (рдмреНрд░рд╣реНрдо рд╕рддреНрдпрдо рдЬрдЧрдиреНрдорд┐рдереНрдпрд╛). When this Nescience isremoved by the light of knowledge, or as the poet here argues,by ┼Ъri Kс╣Ыс╣гс╣Зa, then the individual recognizes the truth. рддрд┐рдЧреНрдорд╛рдВрд╢реБрддрд┐рдЧреНрдорд╛ рдЕрдВрд╢рд╡реЛ рдпрд╕реНрдп рд╕рдГ The sharp-rayed one i. e., the sun. рдХреНрд░реВрд░рдХреНрд▓реЗрд╢рдорд╣реАрд░реБрд╣рд╛рдореНтАФрдХреВрд░рд╛рд╢реНрдЪрддреЗрдХреНрд▓реЗрд╢рд╛рдГ, рддреЗ рдПрд╡ рдорд╣реАрд░реБрд╣рдГ, рддреЗрд╖рд╛рдореНред The trees in the formof painful tortures. рдЙрд░реВрдорд░рдЬреНрд╡рд╛рд▓рд╛рдЬрдЯрд╛рд▓рдГтАФрдЙрд░реБрдорд░ can be linked inthree ways in the context : (i) **рдЙрд░реБрдГ рднрд░рдГрдпрд╕реНрдп рд╕рдГред рдЙрд░реБрдорд░рдГ рдЬреНрд╡рд╛рд▓рд╛рдЬрдЯрд╛рд▓рд╢реНрдЪред**The fire (рд╢рд┐рдЦрд┐) blazing with flames and having a wideexpanse. Vs. 16.**тАФрдХреЛрд╜рдкрд┐тАФSome one i. e. Kс╣Ыс╣гс╣ЗaрдмрдиреНрджреБрд░реНрди рдХрд╛рд░реНрдпрд╕реНрддреНрд╡рдпрд╛тАФ**Markthe manner in which the poet tenders his advice. Just as onewould never enter into friendship with a stranger who islikely to harm one & oneтАЩs friends, so. says the poet, the heartshould not befriend Kс╣Ыс╣гс╣Зa for he is sure to destroy the рд╡рд┐рд╖рдпрд╜which are so dear to it. рдЙрджреНрдЧрд┐рд░рджреНрднрд┐рдГEmitting. Vs. 38.тАФThis stanza marks the beginning of the end ofthe ┼Ъ─Бntavil─Бsa. The following points are to be noted; (i)This stanza and the following ones are sharply different in thenature of their contents from all the preceding stanzas; and, infact, (ii) they have nothing in the connected with the prevailing sentiment of the whole Vil─Бsa. (iii) Tuey all more orless hold together from the point of view of their contents, andform the concluding portion not only of the ┼Ъ─Бntavil─Бsa but ofthe whole Bh─Бmin─лvil─Бsa as well. (iv) These stanzas showJagann─БthaтАЩs high self-consciousness as a poet. They breathedefiance and challenge of other poets, almost curses at thosewho cannot appreciate and acknowledge Jagann─БthaтАЩs worth as& poet, and pontifical sniffs at the humble beginners in therealms of poetry. (v) They refer, again and again, to whatJagann─Бtha looked upon as the one outstanding merit of hispoetry**тАФрдорд╛рдзреБрд░реНрдп.** And lastly, (vi) They are conspicuous for themore or less autobiographical element in them.

the standard which Jagann─Бtha sets for all aspirants forrecognition as poets to conform to.
